iii | P a g e

Wrecks and Obstructions of Lower Laguna Madre ............................................................................10

Abstract
Surveying And Mapping, LLC (SAM) conducted historical research and a marine archaeological survey of
the proposed 72-acre South Padre Island Second Access Project (CSJ: 0921-06-163), near Laguna Madre
in eastern Cameron County, Texas. This work was completed under contract with SWCA of Austin, Texas.
Construction of a bridge across the Lower Laguna Madre is proposed by the Cameron County Regional
Mobility Authority. The area of potential effect (APE) for the marine cultural resources survey is
approximately 7.5 miles long. The construction right-of-way across the bay is 80 feet (24 meters) wide.
The survey corridor width is 394 feet (120 meters) wide and encompasses 371 acres. Prior to initiating
survey investigations, SAM conducted a desktop background review of the project area, which
determined that no previously conducted cultural resources surveys or archaeological sites have been
reported within 1-mile of the APE. SAM’s field investigations consisted of a marine remote-sensing
survey followed by probing of two magnetic anomalies. The survey was completed between June 16 and
21, 2014. Probing was completed from January 27 to 30, 2015. Archaeological assessment of the APE
included portions of Texas General Land Office Mineral Lease Tracts 703, 704, 706, 707, 708, 714, 715,
716, 725 and 726 in Laguna Madre. Only tracts 708, 715 and 716 are designated by the Texas Historical
Commission as having a high probability for the occurrence of historic shipwrecks; however, an
archaeological assessment was conducted for all data acquired from the entire survey. The purpose of
the study was to map potential shipwrecks so they can be avoided by bottom-disturbing activities
associated with construction in the proposed APE. This study was mandated under Section 106 of the
National Historic Preservation Act (Public Law 89-665; 16 U.S.C. 470) and the Antiquities Code of Texas
(Texas Natural Resource Code, Title 9, Chapter 191). The minimum reporting and survey requirements
for marine archaeological studies conducted under a Texas Antiquities Permit are mandated by The
Texas Administrative Code, Title 13, Part 2, Chapters 26 and 28, respectively. SAM encountered no
properties that meet the criteria for listing as a State Antiquities Landmark or for inclusion on the
National Register of Historic Places. Therefore, no further work is recommended within the survey area.

iv | P a g e

I.

Introduction

In June 2014, Surveying And Mapping, LLC (SAM) performed a marine geophysical survey and
archaeological assessment of the South Padre Island 2nd Access Project (Figure 1) crossing the Laguna
Madre for 7.5 miles between Port Isabel and South Padre Island, Texas. The survey was conducted on
behalf of the Cameron County Regional Mobility Authority and HNTB under a subcontract with SWCA.
Work was completed under Texas Antiquities Permit 6885. The purpose of the survey was to determine
whether significant historic sites might be impacted by construction of a proposed bridge across the
Laguna Madre.

Figure 1: Project alignment across Laguna Madre
Cultural resources investigations were required by the Texas Historical Commission (THC) on those
portions of the project comprising the construction corridor where bottom disturbances might affect
shipwrecks resting on, or embedded in, the seafloor. The project right of way is 80 feet (24 meters) wide
centered on the proposed bridge. The archaeological survey corridor measures 394 feet (120 meters)
wide and covers approximately 371 acres. The property to be surveyed is publicly owned and is under
the control of the Texas General Land Office (GLO). The project area (Figure 1) spans portions of GLO
Mineral Lease Tracts 703, 704, 706, 707, 708, 714, 715, 716, 725 and 726. Only tracts 708, 715 and 716
are designated by the Texas Historical Commission as having a high probability for the occurrence of
1|Page

historic shipwrecks; however, an archaeological assessment was conducted for all data acquired from
the entire survey.
The proposed bridge alignment crosses an area having potential for shipwrecks dating back to the
sixteenth century. Prior to harbor improvements of the late nineteenth and early twentieth centuries,
Brazos Santiago Pass was the only naturally deep inlet and protected anchorage on the lower Texas
Coast. Brazos Santiago Harbor became a hub of waterborne transportation and commerce with local
connections through Laguna Madre to Port Isabel and to Brownsville and Matamoros overland and by a
water route up the Rio Grande. The potential for historic shipwrecks within the survey corridor is
elevated by its proximity to Port Isabel and Brazos Santiago Harbor.
Marine archaeological survey was conducted in compliance both with Section 106 of the National
Historic Preservation Act (Public Law 89-665; 16 U.S.C. 470), which requires federally funded or
permitted projects to give due consideration to cultural resources, and with the Antiquities Code of
Texas (Texas Natural Resource Code, Title 9, Chapter 191), which provides for the protection of cultural
resources on state lands. The minimum reporting and survey requirements for marine archaeological
studies conducted under a Texas Antiquities Permit are mandated by The Texas Administrative Code,
Title 13, Part 2, Chapters 26 and 28, respectively.
The archaeological survey was completed between June 16 and 21, 2014. Probing of two unidentified
magnetic anomalies was conducted from January 27 to 30, 2015. No artifacts were collected during
either phase of investigation. The purpose of this work was to assess areas where the bay floor might be
disturbed by construction-related activities. All navigable work areas were surveyed to support an
archaeological assessment of the project by mapping potential shipwrecks, which might be eligible for
nomination to the National Register of Historic Places (NRHP) or as State Antiquities Landmarks, so they
can be avoided by bottom-disturbing activities. Two unidentified magnetic anomalies were initially
recommended for avoidance because of their consistency with anomalies reported over known
shipwrecks; however, both targets were later investigated by probing and discounted as not historically
significant.
This report is organized into five sections that provide the necessary context for interpreting the results
of the archaeological survey. Section II summarizes the maritime background of Lower Laguna Madre,
including its history, a synopsis of previous archaeological investigations in the vicinity, and the potential
for occurrence and preservation of archaeological sites in the study corridor. Section III describes the
methods used for acquisition and analysis of geophysical and probing data. Section IV presents the
results and archaeological recommendations of this study. Finally, a list of all bibliographic references
cited in the text is provided as Section V. Appendix A (not for public disclosure) contains maps of the
survey results. Appendix B contains a table of targets originally recommended for avoidance but
subsequently cleared by probing investigations, including their geographic coordinates. Appendix C
contains a copy of Antiquities Permit 6885.

2|Page

II.

Maritime Background

History
The Gulf Coast of Texas was first documented by the Spaniard Alonso Alvarez de Pineda in 1519 (Alperin
1977:137). Pineda led an expedition to explore and map lands bordering upon the Gulf of Mexico. His
voyage filled the remaining gap in knowledge that eliminated any idea of a route to Asia through the
Gulf of Mexico. His map indicates inlets presumably corresponding to Brazos Santiago Pass and the Rio
Grande; however, there is no solid evidence that he ever explored the Laguna Madre.
Europeans made only occasional forays into Texas for the next two centuries following Pineda’s visit. The
Spanish claimed the region but focused their efforts south of the Rio Grande except when other European
powers showed interest in the area. For example, rumors of a Dutch presence at the mouth of the Rio
Grande served as the impetus for a Spanish military expedition to the area in 1638 (Stambaugh and
Stambaugh 1954:21-23; Ruecking 1955:55). And when the French explorer La Salle established a
foothold on the Texas Coast with the construction of Fort Saint Louis in Matagorda Bay, the Spanish
showed renewed interest in destinations north of the Rio Grande. In an effort to root out the French, Spain
sent a series of expeditions northward from Mexico. One of these expeditions, led by Alonso de Leon in
1687, crossed north of the Rio Grande and passed through present-day Cameron County in search of La
Salle’s fort (Bolton 1921: 214).
Texas lacked permanent European settlements until 1746 (Zavaleta 1986:131). Concerned that French
colonists in Louisiana would move into the area, the King of Spain appointed Jose de Escandon in 1746 to
colonize Nuevo Santander (Pierce 1917:17). He successfully established several towns and missions along
the Rio Grande, although none of his settlements were located near the modern Texas coast. Escandon
thought the coastal plain was uninhabitable; however, within 20 years, migrants from Reynosa and
Camargo established a village, eventually named Matamoros, closer to the mouth of the Rio Grande
(Pierce 1917:20). Other small communities were started near the coast about this same time including the
village of Fronton (modern Port Isabel), which was established during the 1770s (Webb 1952, II: 388, cited
in Gearhart and Moore 1987:II-11) and Brazos de Santiago by 1788 (Pierce 1917:143). Fronton and Brazos
Santiago started as summer resort communities.
Occasional ship traffic through Brazos Santiago Pass and the Lower Laguna Madre likely occurred between
1519 and the 1770s when Fronton was established at modern Port Isabel. Navigation in the bay
presumably occurred on a steadier but slow basis once small settlements at Fronton and Brazos Santiago
were established. From then onward, the density of maritime traffic, and hence the potential for
maritime losses, in Lower Laguna Madre gradually increased, although prior to Mexican independence in
1821, the pass and harbor of Brazos Santiago saw a relatively small amount of shipping.
The harbor at Brazos Santiago was first opened to foreign trade in 1823 when an area rancher shipped
luxury items from New Orleans (Alperin 1977:139 in Espey, Huston & Associates, Inc. [EH&A] 1990:26). It
was during that same year that the Mexican government designated Matamoros as an official port of
entry. Geography led to a commercial bond between the harbor at Brazos Santiago and the town of
Matamoros. Brazos Santiago Pass is the only naturally deep harbor in south Texas, whereas the mouth of
3|Page

the Rio Grande is extremely shallow and hazardous. Ships loaded and unloaded cargo at Brazos Santiago,
and merchandise was transported overland by oxcart and mule to and from Matamoros (Alperin 1977:139
and Ferguson 1976:35 in EH&A 1990:26). Brazos Santiago soon became the most important Mexican port
north of Vera Cruz, and traffic through its harbor increased in step with the rising fortunes of Matamoros.
In 1829, Captain Henry Austin brought his steamship Ariel up the Rio Grande to Matamoros in an effort to
improve upon the transport of goods between Brazos Santiago and Matamoros. His plan was to replace
the overland hauling of merchandise between Brazos Santiago and towns along the Rio Grande (Horgan
1984:481 and Webb 1952, I:67 in EH&A 1990:26). Austin abandoned his plans two years later; however,
the idea would be revisited on a grander scale within 20 years.
Brazos Santiago grew in importance during the Republic of Texas Period and the subsequent U.S. War
with Mexico. The Mexican government maintained a customs revenue station and a military post and
gun battery at Brazos Santiago by 1837 (Hoyt, et al. 1991: Appendix A, Letter 15). The revenue station at
Brazos Island was destroyed by a hurricane in October 1837 (Coast Depot and Shipping Port 1850:7). The
military post was destroyed by "inundation" in 1844 (Hoyt, et al. 1991: Appendix A, Letter 15). Following
the August 1844 hurricane, the Mexican government ordered its officers to Point Isabel, and Brazos was
temporarily abandoned (Coast Depot and Shipping Port 1850:7).
Mexico broke off diplomatic relations with the United States in January of 1846. In February, General
Zachary Taylor was ordered “to take up a position on or near the Rio Grande as soon as possible." In March
Taylor established a new base with 3,554 men at the village of Fronton on Point Isabel (Nichols 1963:42-44
in EH&A 1990: 34). The war with Mexico lasted from March 1846 until February 1848. Both Point Isabel
and Brazos Santiago were utilized by General Taylor and his troops during the war. Thomas Thorpe, one of
Taylor's officers, described the Mexican village of Fronton as "composed of grass-thatched cottages, made
of reeds, or spars of wrecked vessels..." (quoted by Banks 1983:14-15 in EH&A 1990: 34). Taylor named his
camp at Point Isabel, "Fort Polk" after the U.S. President.
Immediately upon the arrival of the detachments of the U.S. troops, Point Isabel
became a place of great business. The few Mexican huts that were left standing
after the fire, were at once occupied; and then commenced the erection of a
variety of rude sheds and dwellings, for the accomodation [sic] of the "military
stores" and the property of the sutlers who followed the army. The harbor [at
Brazos Santiago] was filled with vessels laden with the stores of the army, and of
every possible size, that could enter it... (Thomas Thorpe quoted by Banks
1983:14-15 in EH&A 1990: 35).
Fort Polk was dependent upon the deep harbor at Brazos Santiago Pass for delivery of supplies, so Taylor
established a supply depot on Brazos Island soon after his arrival (Crimmins 1950:204). Ocean-going
vessels drawing as much as 7.5 feet of water could cross the bar at Brazos Santiago Pass, but they were
unable to navigate the 3.5-foot-deep route to Fort Polk at Point Isabel. Supplies intended for Fort Polk
entered the harbor through Brazos Santiago Pass and were unloaded at the supply depot on Brazos Island.
From there they were transferred to shallow-draft vessels for transport to Point Isabel or to the Rio
Grande.
4|Page

The Brazos Santiago supply depot was critical to the Mexican War effort. Major Thomas Eastland,
Quartermaster for the U.S. Army at Brazos Santiago, penned an eye-witness account of Brazos Island
during 1847 and 1848. His report, as transcribed in Hoyt, et al. (1991: Letter 9 of Appendix A), is quoted at
length below in order to demonstrate the massive transformation that the war brought to the Lower
Laguna Madre.
This Depot was the great channel through which passed the troops and supplies
for the Army operating on the upper line and from which was embarked the
returning Army, munitions of war, etc. The accumulation of property [at the
depot], therefore, was immense, and its protection a matter of serious and
anxious consideration. My predecessor [Captain J.M. Still] had commenced
erecting suitable warehouses, and I deemed it my duty to continue until all the
stores (subject to injury from exposure) were safely sheltered. ...houses were
built capable of holding all the stores then exposed on the beach (under cloth
covers) or to arrive. ...Suitable buildings were also erected for officers and
soldiers quarters, mechanics, and others in Government service, and for work
shops, etc.
For giving greater facility to receiving and shipping property, etc. and to save
demurrage...it became necessary to construct wharves, this was done in a very
cheap manner, by using old vessels, no longer fit for service. As many as seven
vessels could be accomadated [sic] at the same time.
A cheap breastwork (of Palmetto logs) was made along the beach in front of the
Public property, which had a good effect in protecting the shore from the wash
of the sea.
The frequent occasion to repair vessels, and the great expense of sending them
abroad for that purpose, made it necessary to establish a ship yard. A set of
ways were built, and all the appertenances necessary for repairing vessels were
procured, this was likewise done at the Mouth of the Rio Grande, there also,
were established a machine shop and foundry, thus giving the means of making
almost every repair required by either steam or sail vessels...
...Steam vessels were kept at Brazos for towing over the bar and at no time
(unless during very bad weather) was public business suspended for want of
means... Within the time I had charge of this Depot 1155 vessels (brigs, barks,
schooners, etc.) were towed in and out of the harbor...
Among other of my receipts and issues of public property may be named
560,786 bushels oats, 115,940 bushels corn, 5,311,714 pounds hay, 86,658
barrels stone coal...
An abundant supply of good water was obtained from the Rio Grande by the
returning wagon trains...
The business of the Depot at Point Isabel (also under my supervision) though
inconsiderable, was satisfactorily conducted (Major Thomas Eastland; Hoyt, et
al. 1991: Appendix A, Letter 9).
5|Page

The U.S. Army maintained a supply depot for a time following the war with Mexico; however, it was not
long before unneeded supplies, equipment and buildings were dispersed to other military posts. Over
time, the numerous buildings and wharves that remained were damaged by storms and neglect. A letter
from 1849 describes the effects of one storm shortly after the conflict had ended:
...a violent storm prevailed here yesterday which caused considerable damage
to public and private property. The fence around the Depot and the stable were
blown down. The "Monmouth" dragged her anchors and went ashore, but was
got off without any damage. Every private vessel in the harbor went ashore,
except a Mexican schooner which sank at her anchors in 20 or 30 feet of water...
(W.W. Chapman 1849 in Hoyt, et al. 1991: Appendix A, Letter 11).
The demise of Brazos Santiago Depot was postponed by an economic boom in the lower Rio Grande Valley
following the war. American control of the Rio Grande’s north bank seemed assured, and entrepreneurs
were set at ease by stabilization of the U.S.-Mexico border.
Gamblers and saloonkeepers from the States swarmed up from Point Isabel and
opened their ships to a rush of custom. Dance halls equipped with musicians
and girls and upstairs rooms, found a large trade...Sutlers came with stores of
goods, to be sold at sky-high prices...shipments of ice arrived, and whiskey
juleps could be had at the bars (Horgan 1984:702-703 in EH&A 1990: 37).
The partnership of M. Kenedy and Co. was formed in 1850 by Captains Mifflin Kenedy and Richard King
both of whom had gained experience navigating the Rio Grande in 1846 when the U.S. Army hired them to
ferry military supplies upriver to Camargo (Vezzetti 1986:77; Pipkin 1940:81). They purchased the army's
steamboats after the war to outfit their venture with the financial backing of longtime local merchant
Charles Stillman and had a virtual monopoly for the next 20 years on trade between Brazos Santiago and
Rio Grande towns such as Matamoros and Brownsville.
The establishment of political stability in the region also happened to coincide with the California gold
rush. Point Isabel and Brownsville, and undoubtedly Brazos Santiago, profited by outfitting expeditions to
the gold fields (McKenna 1956:14 in EH&A 1990: 38). Colonel John S. (Rip) Ford, writing in 1859, stated
that up to ten million dollars of annual merchandise passed through Brazos Santiago and up the Rio
Grande. Morgan Line steamships were visiting the Brazos Santiago wharf every eight or ten days and
transshipping their supplies to Brownsville on a fleet of King and Kenedy steamboats (Ford 1963:269 in
EH&A 1990: 38).
Brazos Santiago Depot had a military function throughout the 1850s. Fort Polk, however, was abandoned
in 1850 (Banks 1983:23 in EH&A 1990: 38). At the outbreak of the Civil War, all U.S. troops stationed at
Brazos Santiago were withdrawn and replaced by 1500 Texas Volunteers (Ford 1963:319 in EH&A 1990:
42). The Confederates controlled the Rio Grande and the southern Gulf coast until late 1863. Union forces
under command of Major General Nathaniel Banks anchored off Brazos Santiago on November 1 of that
year and came ashore the next day with 6,998 men to take Brownsville (Pierce 1917:42-43 and Banks
1983:30 in EH&A 1990: 43).

6|Page

By the close of hostilities, Brazos Island was growing in population and was preparing for a renewal of
commercial shipping. In August 1870, Simon Celaya of Brownsville received a charter for the Rio Grande
Railroad from Point Isabel to Brownsville. Such a railroad would compete directly with Rio Grande
steamboat commerce. King, Kenedy & Company appeared to devise a plan to impede the railroad’s
success. They proposed to rent the Government Wharf and warehouse at Brazos Santiago (Hoyt, et al.
1991: Appendix A, Letter 55). All trade between ocean-going ships and Point Isabel at the time depended
upon these facilities for transferring cargo to shallow-draft vessels. Their proposal was met with stiff
resistant by prominent merchants as far away as New York. Both King, Kenedy and Company and the
Morgan Line steamships already had free use of the wharf, whereas others were charged wharfage.
Furthermore, they already held a virtual monopoly on transshipment of merchandise from Brazos Santiago
up the Rio Grande to Brownsville or across the Laguna Madre to Point Isabel. Celaya was concerned that
King and Kenedy would not allow his company to use the wharf in an effort to keep business away from
the railroad.
The Rio Grande Railroad between Point Isabel and Brownsville did not affect trade at Brazos Santiago; in
fact, it depended on it. Despite the efforts of Kenedy and King to prevent this railroad from competing with
their Rio Grande steamboat business, it went into operation in 1871. However storms in 1873, 1874 and
1886 seriously damaged the railroad causing it to be placed into receivership (Reed 1941:467 and
Ferguson 1976: 208 in EH&A 1990: 70). By then, the Corpus Christi, San Diego and Rio Grande narrowgauge railroad, built by Kenedy, King and Uriah Lott, and completely bypassing Brownsville, had been in
operation for 10 years (Pierce 1917:150 in EH&A 1990: 70).
Although competition with the railroads severely curtailed the use of Brazos Santiago Harbor, there
continued to be at least one wharf on the island, and presumably a warehouse or two, until about 1910.
The River and Harbors Act of 1881 outlined a plan for the construction of parallel jetties to protect the pass
at Brazos Santiago, so a deep-water port could be established. Due to lack of sufficient funding for the
1880s jetty project, the deep-water project at Brazos Santiago remained incomplete for 20 years.
Meanwhile newer and larger ships bypassed Brazos Santiago for deeper ports such as Galveston (Alperin
1977:43 in EH&A 1990: 74). In 1935, the current jetties and the Brownsville Ship Channel were finally
completed.

Previous Investigations
No archaeological surveys have been completed within the marine portion of the study corridor;
however, a number of relevant studies have been completed in the vicinity. Research published in those
reports has summarized much literature covering this area. SAM has reviewed the Texas Archeological
Sites Atlas and available shipwreck databases covering this area to determine whether any marine
archaeological sites or historic shipwrecks have been reported within the Lower Laguna Madre (see
Table 1).
Remains of Brazos Santiago Depot were revealed by a hurricane in 1933. Tent pegs, presumably dating
from the Civil War, were discovered embedded in clay once the hurricane had removed the sand
overburden (Champion 1938). Hurricane Beulah again exposed the site in 1967. THC staff visiting the site
after Beulah documented tent pegs, barrels, trenches, pilings and bricks. A wide variety of artifacts were
7|Page

recovered during this exposure, including clothing, leather footwear, weapons, and assorted other items. A
map of the 1967 collection areas (Corbin and Webb 1967), on file at the THC, is illustrated in Hoyt, et al.
(1991: 60). Brazos Santiago Depot was nominated to the NRHP in 1974 based on the 1967 THC discoveries
(THC 1971). Brazos Santiago Depot was exposed yet again by Hurricane Allen in 1980 (Banks 1983). The
THC mapped additional features and collected more artifacts with assistance from the Texas Parks and
Wildlife Department.
The former Texas Antiquities Committee (TAC) conducted an early magnetometer survey of the Brazos
Santiago Pass area, which was presumably prompted by the remarkable discoveries on Brazos Island
following Hurricane Beulah (Hays and Herrin 1970). The TAC expanded greatly on this initial work with
three field seasons of marine remote-sensing investigations along the Gulf of Mexico side of Padre Island
during the mid-1970s. Their work included extensive magnetometer surveys and diver investigations to
identify magnetic anomaly sources (Arnold 1976, 1978). Their surveys extended along the coast from the
Rio Grande to north of Mansfield Pass. Particular attention once again was paid to the area around Brazos
Santiago Pass because of its intensive maritime history as the only natural deep water port in the region.
The TAC survey crossed the Brownsville Entrance Channel seaward of the jetties but did not cover any part
of Laguna Madre.
A survey was conducted in 1973 to identify and assess cultural resources along the Brownsville Ship
Channel, which was to be widened and deepened as part of the Brazos Island Harbor Waterway Project.
Several sites along the channel were recorded and tested, although no artifacts were recovered from
Brazos Santiago Depot (Hall and Grombacher 1974).
The history of Brazos Santiago Depot and an analysis of the ceramics collected by the THC in 1967 and
1980 formed the basis of a Master of Arts thesis by Cynthia Banks (1983). Updated National Register
Nomination Form continuation sheets were submitted to the National Park Service by the THC (1986)
based on Banks’ thesis and the archaeological field work accomplished between 1967 and 1981. Brazos
Santiago Depot was officially entered into the National Register of Historic Places on March 14, 1986.
EH&A conducted marine investigations of the Brownsville Ship Channel and Brownsville Entrance Channel
in 1980 and 1981 as part of the Brazos Island Harbor Channel Improvement Project. That survey reported
over 200 magnetic anomalies along the channel, 113 of which were classified as significant. Their study
also included a terrestrial magnetometer survey in the area of Brazos Santiago Depot. Some artifacts
exposed by Hurricane Allen were still visible at the time of their work. A total of 866 anomalies were
reported in the depot area. None of the anomalies from either survey were tested to determine their
source (EH&A 1981).
Coastal Environments, Inc. conducted a magnetometer survey in 1987 in order to determine whether
historic shipwrecks would be affected by construction of a proposed small boat channel at Port Isabel
(Pearson 1987). The survey covered an area of Laguna Madre measuring 7,250 x 10,875 feet
immediately adjacent and north of Port Isabel and the existing Queen Isabella Causeway. A number of
magnetic anomalies were recorded during the survey. Many of them were attributed to geologic
features or areas of man-induced disturbances such as dredged material deposits and dredged channels.
8|Page

Others were attributed to modern trash and debris. Magnetic data, combined with historical
documentation of shoreline retreat at Port Isabel, indicated that a considerable segment of the
nineteenth-century town had eroded into the bay. A recommendation was made to avoid a linear area
adjacent the shoreline until a more careful assessment of the area is made.
In 1987, EH&A conducted archaeological and historical assessments of areas proposed for construction of
residential and commercial development along the Rio Grande and on Brazos Island (Gearhart and Moore
1987). A marina proposed on the north shore of Brazos Island was of potential concern for affecting
Brazos Santiago Depot, which had been listed on the NRHP only a year before. A reconnaissance survey
conducted by EH&A in the proposed marina area could not accurately delineate the Brazos Santiago Depot
site boundaries due to the sand overburden; however, a review of existing reports and maps pertaining to
the site, demonstrated the potential for its disturbance and the marina was never built.
In 1989, EH&A conducted a remote-sensing survey of the Brownsville Entrance Channel and the
Brownsville Ship Channel (EH&A 1990). The survey included side-scan sonar, sub-bottom profiler and a
fathometer in conjunction with a magnetometer. A total of 78 magnetic anomalies were reported, of
which 16 were recommended for archaeological avoidance. As the Brownsville Ship Channel was
scheduled for maintenance dredging, 6 anomalies along that channel were investigated by archaeological
divers immediately following the survey. None of those six anomalies proved to be archaeologically
significant.
EH&A conducted additional archaeological testing along the Brownsville Entrance Channel, the Brownsville
Ship Channel and in Back Bay, a small bay that formed during the 20th century on the northern tip of
Brazos Island (Hoyt, et al. 1991). Archaeologists investigated 16 previously reported magnetic anomalies
and evaluated the archaeological potential of Back Bay, which lies within the Brazos Santiago Depot
National Register Property (41CF4). Divers located several submerged historic features in Back Bay,
including an intact railroad siding (minus the wooden ties) and a grid of eroded pilings presumed to be the
remains of a wharf. Research conducted at the National Archives and at the National Ocean Service
Hydrographic Archives gathered a number of historic maps and letters pertaining to Brazos Santiago. Sixty
letters were transcribed in EH&A’s report and historic shoreline overlays were produced from archival
maps.
In 1991, EH&A completed a remote-sensing survey in Back Bay within the boundaries of Brazos Santiago
Depot National Register Property (41CF4), as well as archaeological dive investigations of previously
located magnetic anomalies within the Brownsville Entrance Channel and Back Bay (Hoyt and Gearhart
1992). This project was a follow-up to previous investigations conducted by EH&A (1990) and Hoyt, et al.
(1991). Dive investigations continued on previously mapped magnetic anomalies and on historic features
discovered in Back Bay. Correlation of previous archival cartographic evidence with archaeological
observations suggested that the railroad siding was built in 1864 and that the pilings were part of the
Government Wharf built between 1868 and 1871.
In 2005, Panamerican Consultants, Inc. performed a remote-sensing survey along the east side of the Gulf
Intracoastal Waterway south of the Queen Isabella Causeway (Lydecker 2006). Survey instrumentation
9|Page

included a magnetometer and side-scan sonar. The survey mapped 38 magnetic anomalies and 15 sidescan sonar targets. None of the magnetic anomalies or side-scan targets was considered significant;
therefore, none was recommended for further investigation.

Potential for Historic Shipwrecks
SAM researched potential shipwrecks reported within the Lower Laguna Madre for a distance of about 8
miles to the south and 4 miles to the north of the project area (Table 1). Sources consulted for this
research included the Texas Archaeological Sites Atlas, maintained by the THC, the National Oceanic and
Atmospheric Administration (NOAA) Automated Wreck and Obstruction Information System (AWOIS)
database, and a shipwreck database compiled by PBS&J (now Atkins). The AWOIS list focuses on
shipwrecks and other navigational hazards along the coastal United States. The PBS&J database was
assembled by a group of archaeologists, including this author, while employed by PBS&J and is based in
part on information gathered from charts, historical reports, THC files, and the AWOIS database. The
PBS&J database focuses primarily on well-documented commercial wrecks postdating 1850. It is
important to note that the final deposition of most wrecks has not been ascertained. Historical accounts
of wreck site locations are often inexact and can contain errors based on the original source. Precise
locations of most wrecks are unknown.
Table 1 lists 53 shipwrecks reported in the Lower Laguna Madre. Almost all of these are located several
miles south of the project alignment. They are reported here only to emphasize the large number of
shipwrecks that have occurred in the vicinity, as this number reflects upon the potential for other
unknown wrecks in the bay. Five modern wrecks (marked by asterisks in Table 1) appear on the current
NOAA Chart 11302 within a half mile of the project alignment. These include: AWOIS Wreck 2697, which
was a 16-foot pleasure craft that sank on a spoil bank south of GIWW Marker 58 in 1983 after reportedly
hitting a snag; THC Wreck 1081 charted northwest of GIWW Marker 55; THC Wreck 1083 (incorrectly
reported in THC Atlas as Wreck 1081) charted in a spoil bank west of the GIWW and about 500 feet
north of the project alignment; and two unidentified wreck symbols on the 2011 edition of NOAA Chart
11302, one south of GIWW Marker 57 and one south of a “Platform” symbol near the western end of
the proposed alignment. Positions are approximate for all of these NOAA-charted wrecks.

Factors Affecting Vessel Loss
The main factors influencing the loss of vessels in the survey corridor are the shallow water depth, the
density of vessel traffic and the weather. Historic navigation in the Lower Laguna Madre may have
begun as early as the 16th century and certainly by the 1770s when the village of Fronton was
established on the present site of Port Isabel. Traffic in the bay increased after 1823 when Matamoros
was designated an official port of entry, as Brazos Santiago Pass was the nearest naturally deep pass to
the Rio Grande. When the U.S. war with Mexico began in 1846, the harbor gained national strategic
importance and the volume of maritime traffic accelerated precipitously.
The probability of shipwrecks in the survey corridor is considered relatively low because of the
historically shallow water depths and the project area’s position with respect to historic sites on the
waterfront. Most of the corridor has depths of only 3 to 4 feet. The deepest area is 5 feet. Navigation
14 | P a g e

would have been limited to shallow-draft vessels. Ocean-going ships, common visitors to Brazos
Santiago Harbor, would have grounded far south of the project area had they attempted to go there.
Nevertheless, vessels capable of lightering cargo to Point Isabel could make the trip without running
aground. More important than water depth was the lack of historic commercial destinations in the
Lower Laguna Madre north of Point Isabel prior to the 20th century. Historic references to anchorage of
ships inside of Brazos Santiago Pass suggest that most such activity was concentrated in deeper areas
near the north end of Brazos Island. Vessels crossing the survey corridor were most likely small fishing
vessels or larger shallow-draft vessels blown there by storms.
Stranding is the most common mechanism by which ships are wrecked, accounting for about 64 percent
of total losses reported by the U.S. Lifesaving Service for the period 1876 through 1914 (Gearhart, et al.
1990: Volume IV, 59-61). Two thirds of stranding events were caused by weather. Stranding also is the
most likely source of shipwrecks in the survey corridor. Severe weather was, and remains, the most
significant environmental factor contributing to shipwrecks, accounting for 55 percent of total losses
reported by the U.S. Lifesaving Service for the period 1876 through 1914. Prior to modern weather
forecasting, mariners were hampered by relatively short warnings of approaching storms. Hazardous
weather conditions can occur along the Texas Coast throughout much of the year. Strong northern
winds are generated through the area each winter by the passage of 15 to 20 northers, or rapidly
moving polar fronts, each generally lasting 24–36 hours. Such storms may contain 50-mile-per-hour
winds and generate intense wave activity and long shore currents (McGowen et al. 1976:19–23, 94).
Hurricanes season lasts from late June through October. Hurricanes have winds upwards of 74 miles per
hour and can have devastating effects on any ships caught in their paths.
Factors Affecting Vessel Preservation
Preservation of vessels wrecked within the survey corridor would depend largely upon the composition
of their hull and the degree to which they become and remain buried. In this area, the lower portions of
a wrecked vessel can be expected to become buried soon after sinking due in part to scour and
liquefaction of sediments during storms. Metal vessels remain exposed much longer than wooden ones,
although their lower hulls are equally susceptible to burial.
Any wooden components that are not buried tend to disintegrate relatively quickly. Wood-boring
organisms rapidly consume exposed wood. In shallow water, wood weakened by biological organisms
and water saturation is then disarticulated and laid flat or removed by hurricanes and fishing trawlers
(where trawling is allowed). In areas such as this having copious quantities of weakly consolidated
sediment, significant portions of a wooden hull can become buried, protecting it from biological
degradation. Burial produces an oxygen-deprived environment, which promotes long-term preservation
of wood.
Iron artifacts, on the other hand, corrode five times faster in seawater than when buried on land.
Calcium carbonate forms a concretion around iron artifacts that incorporates surrounding materials
such as rock and sand or even other artifacts. Oxidation can eventually leach out most or all of the iron,
leaving only a carbonate mold of the original artifact (Hamilton 1998).

15 | P a g e

III.

Methodology

SAM’s survey methodology followed the requirements stipulated by the THC. The survey was conducted
from a 19-foot shallow-draft boat. Instrumentation essential to the archaeological survey included an
Edgetech 4125 side-scan sonar (900 kiloHertz [kHz]), a Geometrics G882 marine magnetometer, an
Odom CVM recording fathometer (200 kHz), and a Trimble Differential Global Positioning System (GPS).
Hypack software was used for navigation and for logging of GPS, magnetometer and bathymetry data on
the survey boat. Horizontal positions were based on the Universal Transverse Mercator (UTM)
Coordinate System, Zone 14 North. GPS positions were integrated into sonar imagery using Edgetech’s
Discover Sonar Acquisition Software. Seven parallel transects were surveyed, spaced an average of 66
feet (20 meters) apart, including one on the right-of-way (ROW) centerline. Additional magnetometer
lines were surveyed, as time allowed, over potentially significant anomalies discovered along the
primary transects.
The magnetometer was the primary instrument relied upon for locating and recognizing potential
shipwreck sites. Side-scan sonar imagery was used secondarily to provide verification of magnetic
anomaly sources above the mud line. Submerged sites, if present, would be visible on the side-scan
sonar record if exposed, except in heavily vegetated areas, and would be located solely based on
magnetometer readings if they are buried or concealed by aquatic vegetation. Bathymetry data was
collected as a tool for planning future field investigations, if required, but is not illustrated in this report.
Vessels predating World War II tend to be constructed of wood thus generally are not preserved above
the seafloor. Nevertheless buried portions of such sites can retain a high level of artifact preservation
and historic integrity. No artifacts were collected during this survey.
The magnetometer sensor was towed on the surface 50 feet (15 meters) aft of the survey boat.
Magnetometer data were acquired at 0.2-second intervals and were logged to the navigation computer.
The sensor height above the bottom was generally 4 feet or less. The echo sounder transducer was
located on the starboard side of the vessel forward of the GPS antenna, and was approximately 1.0 foot
(0.3 meters) beneath the surface. Hypack navigation software calculated and recorded position
estimates for the magnetometer and echo sounder in real-time. Line spacing, sample rate, and vessel
speed determined the resolution of magnetic and bathymetric data. The surveys were conducted at
speeds averaging from 3 to 5 knots (3.5–5.8 miles per hour).
The side-scan sonar sensor was deployed from the survey vessel’s bow for the first two days of work and
then was moved to the port side of the boat forward of the GPS antenna for the remainder of the
survey. It was towed just below the bottom of the vessel to allow a view in both directions while
minimizing the likelihood that it would strike the seafloor. The side-scan sonar was set to image the
seafloor for a distance of 82 feet (25 meters) to either side of the survey path. A mosaic image of the
sonar data (Appendix A) was created using Chesapeake’s SonarWiz software. The data were analyzed to
locate and map objects projecting above the bay bottom.
A Microsoft EXCEL spreadsheet was used to process the magnetometer data prior to contouring. The
spreadsheet uses an algorithm to remove diurnal fluctuations. The difference between anomalous
values and the local background field results in a dataset where abnormally high and low magnetic
16 | P a g e

values (anomalies) are centered around zero (the ambient level). This process removes low frequency
trends from the dataset, such as those caused by diurnal variation, geologic gradients, or gradual
changes in water depth while leaving high-frequency variations (anomalies) intact. Diurnal processing
allows visual representation of the data that reflects the polarity of magnetic anomalies.
A contour map of the diurnally-corrected magnetometer data was prepared using Bentley’s Geopak®
digital terrain modeling software at a 5-nanoTesla (nT) contour interval (Appendix A). Magnetometer
data illustrated in Appendix A and text figures below has been thinned to a 1-second interval between
data points. Magnetic amplitudes between +5 nT and 5 nT are considered insignificant for purposes of
marine archaeological interpretations. The 0-nT contour level is omitted from the contour maps to
prevent a cluttered appearance. Positive amplitude in all magnetic contour illustrations is indicated by
red contours. Negative amplitude is indicated by blue contours.

Interpretation of Magnetometer Data
The probable sources for most magnetic anomalies are relatively small items of modern ferrous debris.
Such anomalies tend to be more concentrated in proximity to modern navigation channels and in other
areas of heavy use. Ferromagnetic debris is common in such settings due to the frequency of
commercial and recreational boat traffic. Concentrations of magnetic anomalies also tend to occur in
the vicinity of industrial developments, petroleum wells, and pipelines.
The method used here to interpret unidentified magnetic anomalies is based upon a comparison of
verified shipwreck and debris anomalies as detailed in Gearhart (2011) and summarized below. The
author has assembled, over the past several years, a diverse collection of magnetic anomalies recorded
over 29 verified shipwrecks and many debris sources in an effort to characterize differences between
these two categories of magnetic sources. Shipwrecks included in this dataset encompass a wide variety
of hull compositions (wood, iron, steel, and concrete), propulsion systems (sail, steam-driven
paddlewheels and propellers, oil and diesel screws, and towed or pushed barges), ages (mid-sixteenth to
the mid-twentieth century), and depositional environments (harbor entrances, surf zones, beaches,
marsh, oyster reefs, open bay, and the Gulf of Mexico). These vessels also wrecked in various ways
including by stranding, foundering (sinking), boiler explosions, and burning. Some were even partially
demolished and/or salvaged after wrecking. Based on Gearhart’s (2011) sample, it is possible to
characterize shipwreck and debris anomalies in an empirical fashion.

Complexity
Many archaeologists have attempted to characterize the differences between shipwreck anomalies and
those associated with ferromagnetic debris. Shipwreck anomalies frequently have been described as
appearing “multicomponent” or “complex”, emphasizing the fact that shipwreck anomalies, in certain
contexts, may exhibit multiple dipoles in addition to having unpaired monopolar amplitudes. Garrison et
al. (1989: II, 223) summarized the more common methods used at the time for prioritizing anomalies.
Historic shipwreck anomalies were said to share the following characteristics: multiple amplitude peaks
of differing magnitudes spread over an area greater than 10,000 square meters (2.5 acres), gentle
gradients, and a linear association with anomalies on adjacent transects. Debris anomalies were
17 | P a g e

characterized as typically having single amplitude peaks covering an area of less than 10,000 square
meters, steep gradients, and no alignment of anomalies on adjacent lines.

Horizontal Dimensions
The horizontal size of an anomaly is referred to by many archaeologists as “duration” in reference to
how many seconds a magnetometer registers anomalous readings as it passes overhead in the water
column. Duration is often used by archaeologists as a tool for discriminating potentially significant
anomalies from those more likely to be caused by debris. For example, Linden and Pearson (2014) would
consider an anomaly significant if it has amplitude of at least 50 nT and a duration of 65 feet or more.
Duration obviously depends upon the velocity of the survey boat; therefore this author instead refers to
duration as anomaly width or area exceeding the +/ 5-nT amplitude level. The widths of historic woodhulled shipwrecks and debris anomalies overlap significantly, thus width (or duration) is not a very
useful tool for discriminating between the two categories of anomaly. Most debris anomalies tend to be
no wider than anomalies observed over wood-hulled sailing ships or wood-hulled steamboats; however,
within the category of shipwreck anomalies, there is a 15-fold difference in width between the smallest
wood-hulled sailing vessel and the largest steel tanker. In other words, when it comes to width alone,
anomalies of small wooden ships, which tend to be the most historic, look more like debris anomalies
and bear less resemblance to larger, especially ferrous, shipwrecks.
The most difficult shipwrecks to detect are the small, wooden, generally historic sites, precisely because
their anomalies are in the size range of many debris anomalies. The smallest verified shipwreck anomaly
known to this author (Figure 2) was recorded over the wreck of an early nineteenth-century sailing
vessel, Site 41CL92. The site was shown by divers to measure at least 23 x 52 feet (7 x 15.9 meters) and
is characterized by a large collection of concreted artifacts, iron bar stock, and pig iron ballast (Borgens
2004).
Separating small shipwreck anomalies from debris anomalies in the same size range is complicated and
cannot be done on the basis of size alone. Garrison et al. (1989: II, 223) characterized shipwreck
anomalies as typically being larger than 10,000 square meters; however, if that were the only criterion
used for selecting significant anomalies, all of the wood-hulled sailing ships and all but one of the woodhulled steamboat anomalies known to this author would be excluded. For example, Site 41CL92,
believed to be either a 67-ft New Orleans schooner Hannah Elizabeth that sank in 1835 or the Mexican
naval sloop General Bustamante that sank in 1830 (Borgens 2004), covers an area of only 1,580 square
meters (0.4 acre) out to the 5-nT contour.

Amplitude
Amplitude is even less useful as a tool for differentiating shipwrecks from debris due to the substantial
overlap between shipwreck and debris anomaly amplitudes. The peak-to-peak amplitude of shipwreck
anomalies from Gearhart (2011) averages 270 nT for wood-hulled sailing vessels (n=6); 5,020 nT for
wood-hulled machine-powered vessels (n=7); and 10,386 nT for iron- and steel-hulled vessels (n=12).
Debris anomalies can have amplitude virtually anywhere within the range of shipwreck anomalies.
Linden and Pearson (2014) are being conservative by recommending targets having amplitudes of at
18 | P a g e

least 50 nT as potentially significant. Anomaly amplitude correlates poorly with vessel size because
amplitude depends greatly upon the distance between the magnetometer and the magnetic source.
Anomalies with large amplitude can result from a wooden shipwreck containing a relatively small iron
mass when measured at close range.

Figure 2: Example of a verified shipwreck anomaly, Site 41CL92

Orientation
Despite the diversity of shipwrecks, their anomalies consistently have one characteristic in common with
one another. They are aligned closely with earth’s magnetic field (e.g., Figure 2). In mid-latitudes of the
northern hemisphere, where all of the wrecks discussed by Gearhart (2011) occur, the primary negative
pole of each shipwreck anomaly is located to the north of its positive pole. By contrast, the alignments
of debris anomalies are not limited to one particular orientation.
A simple debris source produces an anomaly dominated by its permanent magnetic field, thus the
anomaly alignment associated with a solitary item of debris is determined by the orientation of the
object on the seabed, and the polar axis of debris anomalies can be oriented along any point of the
compass. On the other hand, shipwrecks are complex anomaly sources comprised of many
ferromagnetic objects. The randomly-oriented permanent magnetic field of each shipwreck component
19 | P a g e

tends to be cancelled by the overlapping fields of other nearby sources. The weaker earth-induced field
of each source is reinforced by the induced fields of all other nearby components, since all of the
induced fields are oriented parallel to earth’s local field lines. As a result, shipwrecks and other complex
sources have anomalies closely aligned to the direction of magnetic north.
The characteristic of anomaly orientation is used to differentiate magnetic anomalies caused by most
ferrous debris from anomalies caused by shipwrecks. Consideration of magnetic anomaly orientation
has potential to eliminate up to about 80 percent of debris anomalies from further archaeological
concern. By chance, about 20 percent of simple debris sources have anomaly orientations overlapping
those of complex sources, including shipwrecks. At present there is no consistent method for eliminating
that 20 percent of debris from consideration as a potential buried shipwreck.
Many anomalies, however, can be eliminated from consideration as potential shipwrecks simply by
demonstrating that their orientations differ substantially from the direction of magnetic north. The only
cases in which anomalies of simple debris sources should align with earth’s magnetic field is when, by
chance, their magnetic moments, as determined by their orientation on the seafloor, closely align with
magnetic north. The opposite case, a wood-hulled shipwreck with a magnetic anomaly not aligned with
magnetic north, is virtually impossible, as it would require a large percentage of a wreck’s many
ferromagnetic components, by chance, to have the same magnetic moment.
Interpretation of magnetic anomalies, in practice, was done by comparing the magnetometer data,
contoured at a 5-nT interval, to the anomaly of a small wood-hulled historic shipwreck, 41CL92, shown
in Figure 2. The verified 41CL92 shipwreck anomaly was contoured using the same parameters as the
survey data from this project and was properly oriented and scaled to match the data to which it was
compared. Any anomalies that could not have a similar polar orientation, assuming a sufficient data
density, were excluded from further consideration. Anomalies remaining as shipwreck candidates were
then researched with the Texas Railroad Commission to determine which ones could be attributed to
abandoned petroleum wells, which also happen to closely resemble shipwreck anomalies.

Investigating Magnetic Anomalies
Two magnetic anomalies (Anomalies 1 and 2), which were consistent with anomalies observed over
verified shipwrecks, were investigated further to determine whether either was historic in nature. The
primary method used to accomplish this task was by probing across the central portion of each anomaly.
A sufficient number of probes were placed to verify the presence/absence of buried shipwrecks. The
scope of probing investigations was determined in consultation with the State Marine Archaeologist
(email exchange between the author and Amy Borgens on October 21, 2014). Each probe was required
to penetrate the seafloor to a depth of 10 feet below the water surface or until the probe would not
penetrate further due to a hard sediment layer, such as clay. The position of each probe was mapped
using Real-Time Kinematic (RTK) GPS receiving Virtual Reference Station corrections. If an object or
wreck was detected, an effort was to be made to delineate its horizontal and vertical limits.
A MUL-1 hand-held gradiometer (Figure 3) was utilized, where water depth allowed wading, in an
attempt to pinpoint ferromagnetic objects prior to probing. Probing was conducted using a 10-foot-long
20 | P a g e

steel rod with a T-handle and a sliding hammer weight attached to the top end. Probe locations were
pre-selected on Anomaly 1 and temporarily staked with wooden lathes (Figure 4). Anomaly 2 was too
deep and the bottom too soft to allow wading, so the entire probing process was conducted from a boat
while drifting slowly back and forth over the anomaly.

Figure 3: Magnetic Underwater Locator/Gradiometer (MUL-1), SubSurface Instruments, Inc.

Figure 4: Probing grid staked on Anomaly 1
21 | P a g e

IV.

Conclusions

Results
The marine archaeological survey discovered two magnetic anomalies, designated Anomalies 1 and 2
(Figures 5 and 6; Appendices A and B), that bear resemblance to the magnetic signature typically
recorded over shipwrecks (Figure 2, for example). As mentioned above, the characteristic signature of a
shipwreck anomaly is a prominent north-aligned magnetic dipole, centrally located over the hull of the
sunken vessel. The axis between the positive and negative poles of such a dipole should align closely
with the local direction of magnetic north, with the negative pole (blue contours) being located north of
the positive pole in mid-northern latitudes. Smaller outlying anomalies are possible in instances where a
hull has been broken and its contents scattered. No sonar targets were associated with either anomaly.
Anomaly 1 (Figure 5) bears a resemblance to other shipwreck anomalies and could not be ruled out
from consideration as a wreck based on the geophysical data alone. While Anomaly 1 is located in a
dredge spoil area, which is a common place to encounter modern ferromagnetic debris, it is also near
two reported wrecks, both of which appear on the current NOAA Chart 11302. AWOIS Wreck 2697 was
a 16-foot pleasure craft that sank on a spoil bank in 1983 after reportedly hitting a snag. It is charted
about 800 feet south of Anomaly 1. The position reported for THC Wreck 1083 (incorrectly reported in
THC Atlas as Wreck 1081) correlates closely with a wreck symbol on the current edition of NOAA Chart
11302, which is charted about 500 feet north of Anomaly 1. This vessel wrecked prior to 1977, but no
other information is available on the THC Site Atlas. A recommendation was made following the survey
to conduct further investigations in the form of probing.

Figure 5: Anomaly 1 magnetic contours

22 | P a g e

Anomaly 2 (Figure 6) is smaller in size than expected for a typical historic commercial sailing vessel;
however, there is potential for this anomaly to be larger than observed due to the gaps between survey
lines. There are no documented wrecks known to have been reported in the vicinity of Anomaly 2;
however, it could not be ruled out from consideration as a wreck based on the geophysical data alone. A
recommendation was made following the survey to conduct further investigations of Anomaly 2 in the
form of probing.
Two linear magnetic anomalies were discovered near the western end of the proposed causeway, which
are believed to be associated with pipelines. These anomalies (Figure 7) are labeled accordingly in
Appendix A. The easternmost of these anomalies is located near a platform symbol on NOAA Chart
11302. This platform is no longer visible in the field. The westernmost linear anomaly corresponds to a
continuous linear feature on the side-scan sonar mosaic, indicating that a pipe is exposed above the
seafloor.
The side-scan sonar data did not contain any targets of archaeological interest. Heavy concentrations of
submerged aquatic vegetation were observed in spoil areas along the GIWW and throughout the
eastern half of the survey corridor. While the vegetation could be expected to obscure sonar targets of
interest, should they occur, the eastern half of the survey area was also nearly void of magnetic
anomalies. Gaps in sonar acquisition occurred in shoals less than 2 feet deep at the shore approaches on
each end of the survey corridor and in a spoil area on the west side of the Gulf Intracoastal Waterway.

Figure 6: Anomaly 2 magnetic contours

23 | P a g e

Pipeline
Anomalies

Figure 7: Linear magnetic anomalies interpreted as pipelines
Anomaly 1 was further investigated on January 28 and 29, 2015 to determine whether or not it was
associated with a shipwreck. Water depth over the anomaly was 1-3 feet and the bottom was firm,
allowing for easy wading. A series of 11 parallel transects were walked over the central area of the
anomaly using a hand-held gradiometer. Only three isolated magnetic targets, indicated in Figure 8 as
solid black dots, were located using this method, although a broad magnetic trend, as suggested by the
magnetic contours, was apparent using this instrument. The isolated targets were clearly not linear and
exhibited extremely narrow peaks, suggesting they were caused by narrow objects buried near the
surface. All three targets are consistent with what would be expected from wire rope (steel cable),
which is a common source of magnetic anomalies in similar contexts.
The sediment over Anomaly 1 was probed systematically to a depth of 10 feet below the water surface
using a steel rod. A total of 217 probes were placed on a pre-staked grid extending roughly 160 feet in
an east-west direction and 100-140 feet north-south over the central portion of the anomaly. Probes
were placed an average of 10 feet apart. Locations of each probe, relative to the magnetic anomaly, are
illustrated in Figure 8. The position of each probe was recorded using RTK GPS. All probes penetrated to
a depth 10 feet without meeting refusal other than an occasional firm sediment layer at a depth of 3-4
feet below the water surface. The results of probing proved conclusively that a shipwreck is not present
within 10 feet of sea level. Based on the combined probing and gradiometer results, the source of
Anomaly 1 is now assumed to be multiple, compact, ferromagnetic debris objects with the most likely
candidate being steel cable discarded in this dredge spoil area.

24 | P a g e

Figure 8: Anomaly 1 probing results
Anomaly 2 was further investigated on January 29, 2015 to determine whether or not it was associated
with a shipwreck. Water depth over the anomaly was about 4.5 feet deep and the bottom was soft, so
wading and pre-staking the probing grid were not possible. The sediment over Anomaly 2 was probed
systematically to a depth of 10 feet below the water surface using a steel rod. A total of 109 probes
were placed opportunistically as the boat drifted slowly back and forth across the anomaly. The area of
probing extended roughly 50 feet in an east-west direction and 65 feet north-south over the central
portion of the anomaly. Care was taken to include an area north and west of the reported magnetic
anomaly, because this area fell between magnetometer survey transects. Probes were placed 3 to 10
feet apart. Locations of each probe, relative to the magnetic anomaly, are illustrated in Figure 9. The
position of each probe was recorded as it penetrated the sediment using RTK GPS. All probes penetrated
to a depth 10 feet without meeting refusal. The sediment in this area was significantly softer than at
Anomaly 1. The results of probing proved conclusively that a shipwreck is not present within 10 feet of
sea level. Based on the probing results, the source of Anomaly 2 is now assumed to be a compact object
of ferromagnetic debris.

Recommendations
Marine archaeological survey was conducted in compliance both with Section 106 of the National
Historic Preservation Act (Public Law 89-665; 16 U.S.C. 470), which requires federally funded or
permitted projects to give due consideration to cultural resources, and with the Antiquities Code of
Texas (Texas Natural Resource Code, Title 9, Chapter 191), which provides for the protection of cultural
resources on state lands. The minimum avoidance requirements for marine archaeological studies
conducted under a Texas Antiquities Permit are mandated by The Texas Administrative Code, Title 13,
25 | P a g e

Part 2, Chapters 28, Rule 28.6, which states that bottom-disturbing activities must be avoided within 50
meters of potentially significant targets (150 meters for targets located farther than 3 nautical miles
beyond the Gulf of Mexico beach).

Figure 9: Anomaly 2 probing results
Two magnetic anomalies discovered by this survey were initially interpreted as potentially significant
because of their possible association with historic shipwrecks. Anomaly 1 is centered on the proposed
80-ft-wide (24-meter) construction ROW. Anomaly 2 is located immediately north of the proposed ROW.
These two anomalies could not be discounted based on the geophysical data at hand thus SAM
recommended probing in order to determine the presence or absence of a buried shipwreck at either
location. Further investigation by probing demonstrated conclusively that a shipwreck is not present
within 10 feet of sea level at either anomaly. SAM recommends that both Anomaly 1 and Anomaly 2 be
cleared from further cultural resources consideration. This survey discovered no other potentially
significant targets; therefore, SAM recommends cultural resource clearance for the entire marine APE.
As with any such project, it is possible, however unlikely, that undetected cultural resources might be
encountered during construction, particularly at depths greater than 10 feet below sea level. In the
event that potential historic materials are encountered anywhere during construction, work should be
halted within 164 feet (50 meters) of the discovery, and the THC should be notified immediately.
